<span id="freeTextContainer4430505003544382330">'She liked books more than anything else...'<br/><br/>Alongside The Secret Garden, A Little Princess is one of my favourite books. One of those ones where every page seems to resonate completely, and one that I've read so many times that I've lost count. It has never lost its magic. <br/><br/>Sara Crewe is one of the characters I hold most dear, out of all the books I've ever read, she is one of the few that seems to have maintained a permanent residence in my heart and mind. I identified fiercely with her as child</span>
  <span id="freeText4430505003544382330" style="display:none">'She liked books more than anything else...'<br /><br />Alongside The Secret Garden, A Little Princess is one of my favourite books. One of those ones where every page seems to resonate completely, and one that I've read so many times that I've lost count. It has never lost its magic. <br /><br />Sara Crewe is one of the characters I hold most dear, out of all the books I've ever read, she is one of the few that seems to have maintained a permanent residence in my heart and mind. I identified fiercely with her as child, and that feeling remains, even though I am now so much older than her. She is wonderful, dignified, and strange.<br /><br /> I was fascinated with the doll her father gives her early on in the book, Emily, with her custom-made outfits and excessive finery. He is ludicrously wealthy and indulges her absurdly, but Sara is so gracious and aware of the luck of her position in life that it doesn't feel like poor parenting, just the actions of a devoted father with a lot of money to spend! She could so easily be spoiled, but isn't. In many ways she is the serious adult, while he is the excitable child. <br /><br />I won't detail the plot, but despite knowing it by heart I get completely absorbed by it every time I read. I love everything about it. Sara is a wonderfully good child, but she never feels false.<br /><br />'Never did she find anything so difficult as to keep herself from losing her temper when she was suddenly disturbed while absorbed in a book. People who are fond of books know the feeling of irritation which sweeps over them at such a moment. The temptation to be unreasonable and snappish is one not easy to manage.'<br /><br />That quote is probably one of many reasons why she feels like such a kindred spirit to me!<br />💎👸👳💖🐀🐵🍞🌹👑📚📖💟🐒</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Saadia B. || CritiConscience " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Saadia B. || CritiConscience " href="/user/show/76507338-saadia-b-criticonscience">Saadia B. || CritiConscience </a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2486791914" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1582739213213666689">Short story about a little girl named Sara aged 7 who used to live with her father in India. Father being concerned of her education, decides to send her to a boarding school in London. There she lives in luxury than other pupils and everyone was envied because of her intelligence and luxurious style. However her father, Captain Crewe dies from a high fever, leaving Sara penniless. He had put all his money in the diamond mines business along with his friend, who went missing after seeing some lo</span>
  <span id="freeText1582739213213666689" style="display:none">Short story about a little girl named Sara aged 7 who used to live with her father in India. Father being concerned of her education, decides to send her to a boarding school in London. There she lives in luxury than other pupils and everyone was envied because of her intelligence and luxurious style. However her father, Captain Crewe dies from a high fever, leaving Sara penniless. He had put all his money in the diamond mines business along with his friend, who went missing after seeing some losses. <br /><br />The news of Captain Crewe’s death changed Sara’s life completely in the utter misery and mercy of Miss Minchin. She was made to live like a maid who worked for hours, ran errands and taught young pupil lessons. She was a very bright child and a quick learner, who loved reading and creating stories. Her father’s friend who ran away, searched for Sara everywhere and finally finds her after two years, as he happened to move to the house next to the boarding school. <br /><br />He tells her the entire story and gives her every comfort she deserved as she was the owner of half the fortunes from diamond mines, which were later discovered. Sara always pretended and acted as if she was a princess and in the end with the help of magic (she liked to believe that) she lives her life as a princess, who was kind, well mannered, caring and above all loved sharing her goodness with others.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1582739213213666689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1684918790" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1684918790" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1170391-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590218363p2/1170391.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1684918790?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/1170391-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780141341712" href="/book/show/13338967-a-little-princess">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=childrens-book">childrens-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1170391-sara?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1684918790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16416048086563355538">I went into A Little Princess with a small amount of backstory - I’d loved the 1995 film as a child (the protagonist is called Sara...I’m called Sara...it was a given I’d love it) so I had some idea of the plot and characters. However, I had no idea just how much I would be affected by the story. By the last 50 pages, I was sobbing. <br/><br/>A Little Princess is the story of Sara Crewe, who has arrived in London after a privileged life in India as the daughter of Captain Crewe, to start her education at </span>
  <span id="freeText16416048086563355538" style="display:none">I went into A Little Princess with a small amount of backstory - I’d loved the 1995 film as a child (the protagonist is called Sara...I’m called Sara...it was a given I’d love it) so I had some idea of the plot and characters. However, I had no idea just how much I would be affected by the story. By the last 50 pages, I was sobbing. <br /><br />A Little Princess is the story of Sara Crewe, who has arrived in London after a privileged life in India as the daughter of Captain Crewe, to start her education at Miss Minchin’s seminary for girls. Sara is a precocious little girl, often described as ‘queer’ in her opinions and statements, with a vivid imagination who is able to capture the heart of her peers through stories. Being so precocious, I thought I wouldn’t warm to Sara at all - but I was wrong. She’s utterly charming, polite and good. The model child - but told in a believable way. She’s not just a one dimensional character, and as we see her circumstances change, I especially came to love Sara and her plight. Through every adversity she tries to see the good in everything, and everyone. Mostly. Her relationship with the other girls, and her father, further highlight how special she is and how she treats everyone the same - regardless of status and circumstance. <br /><br />The writing is surprising easy to follow and read for a book over 100 years old, and the plot kept me fully engaged throughout. I was blown away with just how besotted I was with the whole story. If anything, I would have liked some expansion on the Minchin sisters to make them more rounded as characters, as I found Amelia in particular quite one line as the simpering younger sister. It made her character arc less impactful than it could have been. <br /><br />All in all, a wonderful children’s classic with touching characters. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16416048086563355538" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_924438337" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/924438337" />
    <a title="Dhanaraj Rajan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan"><img alt="Dhanaraj Rajan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433251321p2/4373783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/924438337?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dhanaraj Rajan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dhanaraj Rajan" href="/user/show/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan">Dhanaraj Rajan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141321127" href="/book/show/2302797.A_Little_Princess">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=children-s-lit">children-s-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=literature-uk">literature-uk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer924438337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11505184221422436374"><b>The Story:</b><br/><br/>It is a simple heart warming story of a rich girl whose fortunes play hide and seek with her. Initially she is very rich and enjoys everything as a princess and after some time we see her reduced to a pauper, having nothing and no body in the world. She works and struggles to support her life as a charity pupil. And then arrives the fairy tale ending in which the pauper is restored to unimaginable riches.<br/><br/><b>What I understood from the Story/the Moral of the Story:</b><br/><br/>1. The nature of life and </span>
  <span id="freeText11505184221422436374" style="display:none"><b>The Story:</b><br /><br />It is a simple heart warming story of a rich girl whose fortunes play hide and seek with her. Initially she is very rich and enjoys everything as a princess and after some time we see her reduced to a pauper, having nothing and no body in the world. She works and struggles to support her life as a charity pupil. And then arrives the fairy tale ending in which the pauper is restored to unimaginable riches.<br /><br /><b>What I understood from the Story/the Moral of the Story:</b><br /><br />1. The nature of life and its movement can never be understood. Life can treat you like a prince/princess this moment and in an instant it can drag you down to the dust, leaving you a pauper. What is asked of us is not to be proud of the riches or not to be grieving for the loss. Instead, we are asked to live the present with all the life that we have. Life itself is better gift than anything else. If you are sent to the attic, learn to enjoy the sunset and the friendships of sparrows and rats.<br /><br />2. Friends are the precious gems not to be missed in this life. Friends will hold you on in any moment and in every moment. They will be ready to face the risks just to meet you and share a joke with you. If loneliness is the most possible villain for life, the friends can play the part of villains for the loneliness.<br /><br />3. Believe in the healing power of imagination. Believe in it and believe in it always. Try to 'suppose' or 'imagine' the happiness in the moments of sadness. I have tried out such possibilities many times. I am not sure I will be able to imagine happiness in my extreme sadness, but this is the lesson from this book. It is, however, not a call to live in the hallucination. Work hard to support your life and in spite of that if you confront troubles and moments of difficulties, seek the soothing power of the imagination.<br /><br />4. Respect everyone in any state of life that you find yourself in. Do not change yourself according to the fortunes. Have respect for everyone and every living being (even for rats). Let not fortunes or emotions decide your behaviour.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11505184221422436374" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89499058?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee" href="/user/show/1603786-lee">Lee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1603786-lee?shelf=unfinished">unfinished</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1603786-lee?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89499058" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2660376123450869172"><i>"she was always dreaming and thinking odd things and could not herself remember any time when she had not been thinking things about grown-up people and the world they belonged to. She felt as if she had lived a long, long time."</i><br/><br/>I wished Frances Hodgson Burnett stopped explaining about Sara at this point. Because at this point, I like her already. Because at this point, I wish I read this book when I was a child. Because at this point, I start to think that Sara and I have similarities.<br/><br/>Sadly tho</span>
  <span id="freeText2660376123450869172" style="display:none"><i>"she was always dreaming and thinking odd things and could not herself remember any time when she had not been thinking things about grown-up people and the world they belonged to. She felt as if she had lived a long, long time."</i><br /><br />I wished Frances Hodgson Burnett stopped explaining about Sara at this point. Because at this point, I like her already. Because at this point, I wish I read this book when I was a child. Because at this point, I start to think that Sara and I have similarities.<br /><br />Sadly though, couple pages later turned my feelings to Sara 180 degrees. She is perfect. Far, far, far too perfect. Not as a child, as a human being. Whimsically pretty, kind, selfless, rich, adored, and different. Boringly perfect. Unlovable perfect.<br />The kind of perfect that leaves me with a feeling:<br />"okay, she got everything, everyone love her, life will turn perfect for her yadda yadda yadda. So why should I give a damn about her?"<br /><br />But of course, this is just temporary. I'm not even halfway reading it. Time will tell, hopefully.<br />*shrugs*</span>

<div id="review_1133732246" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1133732246" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1133732246?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141321127" href="/book/show/2302797.A_Little_Princess">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-early">20th-centurylit-early</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=young-adult-n-kids">young-adult-n-kids</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1133732246" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14871712525487436332">About six months ago I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/980977911' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Secret Garden</a> for possibly the first time, and while I was happy to have finally read it, realized that I probably would have appreciated it more if I had read it as a younger reader.<br/><br/>I felt similarly about <i>A Little Princess</i>.<br/><br/>In this story we have young Sara Crewe, a precocious and intelligent little girl whose father indulges her every whim and desire, sent to a seminary for young ladies where she uses her imagination to make friends and some enemies. When her fath</span>
  <span id="freeText14871712525487436332" style="display:none">About six months ago I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/980977911" rel="nofollow noopener">The Secret Garden</a> for possibly the first time, and while I was happy to have finally read it, realized that I probably would have appreciated it more if I had read it as a younger reader.<br /><br />I felt similarly about <i>A Little Princess</i>.<br /><br />In this story we have young Sara Crewe, a precocious and intelligent little girl whose father indulges her every whim and desire, sent to a seminary for young ladies where she uses her imagination to make friends and some enemies. When her father dies (not a spoiler, it's on the back cover, calm down), Sara is left in the hands of Miss Minchin, the nasty headmistress who already doesn't like Sara because... well, who knows <i>why</i> exactly. In these sorts of books, one never needs a real motive.<br /><br />Because Sara's father is gone, and the money is gone, Miss Minchin takes to hating on little Sara even harder, tossing her in the attic in a room next to the scullery-maid's and making Sara into a little slave of sorts. And times are sad.<br /><br />But never fear! Sara continues using her imagination and playing pretend and making friends with the rats in the walls. Due to her persistence and chronic do-gooding, things wind up turning out pretty well for Sara, in spite of everything, not that I was particularly worried. Sara is the sort of character for which things just naturally work out.<br /><br />So, like <i>The Secret Garden</i> this is a fine little book with a nice moral for younger readers. As an adult reader, and one with a crusted up little black heart, I have bigger issues with these stories, social issues like the way anyone Not White is stereotyped and how some people are worthy and some are not, but everyone should <i>aspire</i> to improve their position in life which is not the same as aspiring to improve oneself.<br /><br />The parts I did appreciate about the story are the same things most of us here appreciate: Sara is a bibliophile and sort of socially awkward in her unconventional ways. She stands her ground in the face of adversity and pisses people off because she doesn't raise her voice when they're screaming at her. I mean, yeah. I get it.<br /><br />I also appreciated the inclusion of the rats. I fell a little in love with Melchisedec and am campaigning for my boyfriend to change one of his rats' names to that, a hard battle considering his rats are all females. I think rats tend to not have good representation in literature, so I was pleased to see Sara befriending them. I didn't do the same when I had mice in my apartment once upon a time, that was downright traumatic and awful and I don't think I slept well for almost a full year. But rats are a lot of fun - the pet kind. (Or the ones who sport capes and have adventures.) Not the jumbo-sized sewer rats you see digging in the trash in Baltimore.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14871712525487436332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('nqtQPDHu3FfV7Hz5WDqGspWbZywy7tvSqXuEJZCM6JWkjTnuirAVP3LHyJivz4xtQ8SA5PxjVbXyBcKUXc8aDw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KeNjXuHmQ37UtGyjC7rUSzqrZesOFRaIrwrq7rYfd7QTxQqMWriKFnOf2ML8T96U7PSCI8CYmO/0dKxfe1yFLg==')})
  }

This is intensified by her tempestuous relationship with the mill-owner and self-made man, John Thornton, as their fierce opposition over his treatment of his employees masks a deeper attraction.\n\nIn North and South, Elizabeth Gaskell skillfully fuses individual feeling with social concern, and in Margaret Hale creates one of the most original heroines of Victorian literature.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7338240982031775352\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2041.Frances_Hodgson_Burnett">About Frances Hodgson Burnett</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2041.Frances_Hodgson_Burnett"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1197934848p3/2041.jpg);'></div>

<a href="/author/show/2041.Frances_Hodgson_Burnett">Frances Hodgson Burnett

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10214722477842052616">Frances Eliza Hodgson was the daughter of ironmonger Edwin Hodgson, who died three years after her birth, and his wife Eliza Boond. She was educated at The Select Seminary for Young Ladies and Gentleman until the age of fifteen, at which point the family ironmongery, then being run by her mother, failed, and the family emigrated to Knoxville, Tennessee. Here Hodgson began to write, in order to sup</span>
  <span id="freeText10214722477842052616" style="display:none">Frances Eliza Hodgson was the daughter of ironmonger Edwin Hodgson, who died three years after her birth, and his wife Eliza Boond. She was educated at The Select Seminary for Young Ladies and Gentleman until the age of fifteen, at which point the family ironmongery, then being run by her mother, failed, and the family emigrated to Knoxville, Tennessee. Here Hodgson began to write, in order to supplement the family income, assuming full responsibility for the family upon the death of her mother, in 1870. In 1872 she married Dr. Swan Burnett, with whom she had two sons, Lionel and Vivian. The marriage was dissolved in 1898. In 1900 Burnett married actor Stephen Townsend until 1902 when they got divorced. Following her great success as a novelist, playwright, and children's author, Burnett maintained homes in both England and America, traveling back and forth quite frequently. She died in her Long Island, New York home, in 1924.<br /><br />Primarily remembered today for her trio of classic children's novels - <i>Little Lord Fauntleroy</i> (1886), <i>A Little Princess</i> (1905), and <i>The Secret Garden</i> (1911) - Burnett was also a popular adult novelist, in her own day, publishing romantic stories such as <i>The Making of a Marchioness</i> (1901) for older readers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10214722477842052616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
